### Post by BigSilly on 2011-10-13
> **ikt said:**
> Gooder than 11.04.

It is. :)

It's certainly nice to be back on Ubuntu after having to leave for a while. Natty just didn't work for me, but so far barring a couple of minor things the Ocelot is proving massively entertaining! I'm really just enjoying having it around. There's still a couple of little niggles, such as launchers on the bar which seem to hang occasionally on right click or when moving them around, and the odd glitch with workspaces, but on balance there's little or nothing that I'd get upset about, and I'm sure fixes will come.

The biggest surprise for me is how far Unity has come. It feels like a finished product now, and I'm sure it'll get even better over time.

### Post by Penguinnerd on 2011-10-13
I'm trying it on LiveUSB with no intent to upgrade from 10.04 any time soon.

I'm very pleasantly surprised. Unity feels more usable than it has in past releases. It's quick enough for me, and I think I could get used to it.

That being said, and with no disrespect to the developers whatsoever, I still thing it was a horrible idea to design unity this way. (But Gnome 3 is no better...) I still don't want to upgrade from 10.04 yet.

Doing their own fork of Gnome 2 would have been a better idea than re-inventing the wheel.

But I want to end my post on a high note: Considering this is Unity, 11.10 is a job well done overall, IMO. ;)

### Post by dave2001 on 2011-10-13
Not real impressed. I hated Unity in 11.04, as did many others, so I think the move to drop classic gnome shell from the default install may have been a mistake. Easy enough to install afterwords.. even if it was a bit buggy.. First time I booted into gnome shell everything was in Chinese.. I'm not even really all that happy about the changes in gnome 3. I guess I'll get used to it, and find out where things are, but at a first glance, it seems to be more trouble to configure a lot of things.

Also this is a big issue in my opinion.. Laptop is running about 5-10 degrees hotter on 11.10. Haven't got good battery life info yet, but I'm expecting it to be worse if I'm running that much hotter. That seems expected according to this article about power usage in 11.10 vs 11.04 [http://www.phoronix.com/scan.php?page=news_item&px=OTg5Mg](http://www.phoronix.com/scan.php?page=news_item&px=OTg5Mg)

### Post by gnomeuser on 2011-10-13
I am deeply disappointed. I have not even been able to make this install on my machine (though this is a MacMini5.2 so kind of a harder target than usual old style BIOS setups, and it has hybrid graphics as well which isn't well supported).

On top of that GNOME3 isn't complete (mising applications such as gnome-documents) with no one step way of installing GNOME3 instead of Unity (hoping someone will produce a reasonable ISO that will install just GNOME3 with correct branding and artwork).

I dislike lightdm, the switch to thunderbird and the omission of systemd as even an installable option.

Infact I would rate this as my least favorite Ubuntu release ever.

On the plus side it does seem to run a little nicer on the ****** arm netbook I bought than previous releases. Still no 3D acceleration though so it is basically still pretty useless, also it is still dead slow on that hardware.

---

Though I will add, I'm not that surprised to hear it. It's inevitable that Ubuntu will focus first and foremost on Unity and Ubuntu's features ahead of Gnome 3 and shell inclusion.

I disagree about Thunderbird personally, as it's always been my email client of choice whatever OS I'm using. I also disagree about LightDM, which so far to me is neat and quick, and pretty too.

### Post by Retlol on 2011-10-13
The out of the box Ubuntu with Unity is horrible. I didn't try 11.04 (I presume it was worse) but I tried Unity for over an hour.

It's horribly slow. To complicated. It has annoying "bugs" (like you can't type to search if you open the launcher, you have to go to applications first to be able to type). There's more, I cba to type it all.

I had to throw Gnome-shell over it and now it runs pretty smooth. The UI is much more pleasant to use as well. 

The lack of tools to change stuff is strange as well. Gnome 2.x wasn't know to have lots of guis for changing stuff, but in Ubuntu 11.10 there's nothing. Not even all the basics are covered.

Ubuntu has been touting to have designers for years but basic stuff like the app with all the system settings and stuff on is just white background with some ugly icons (even something simple like a light gradiant would do wonders and takes like 30 seconds to make). 

I tried their new software center and while it looks good, it's slow, to slow.

The installer crashed when I tried to install Ubuntu. It didn't the 2nd time I tried with the same disc (well, usb stick).

---

### Post by Munk3y on 2011-10-13
Been running it on 5 computers (2 which are Laptops) for a week and they all work great. Even on one at work joined to our Windows Domain right now with Likewise-Open.

The problems I've had:

-MSI Wind Netbook shuts down/hibernates on pulling power cord. Fixed it by using dconf-tools to turn off "use-time-for-policy".

-Have to login to a local user vs domain user to add a Network Printer.

Other than that, looks great and works great. If Boxee ever upgrades their software, I may install it on my Media Center too. I'm on 11.04 there still, don't feel like messing with it.

---

### Post by motrengaw on 2011-10-14
Unity is the straw that broke the camel’s back for me.  I am actively  shopping for other Linux distros to replace the 10.4 LTS on my main  desktop when it sunsets.  I’ve tried Unity under 11.04 and 11.10 on  ancillary machines in my home network, and replaced it with Gnome  Classic after a few tiresome days.  It is a stupid, slow, and  ineffective toy: pretty looking but inefficient.   The learning curve is  possibly lower for someone coming to computers from a smartphone or  game console background, but for serious users it’s assumptions about  how to organize tasks and its severe truncation of application  categories are tiresome in the extreme.


Cannonical have stated that, despite a tidal wave of discontent with Unity, that this is their 'new direction' to which they are 'fully committed' and that the classic desktop will eventually disappear.  Hence -my- commitment to look elsewhere for a distro that doesn't waste my time and inflame my tendinitis with 'flavor-of-the-month' GUIs.



 This and the incompetent PulseAudio integration have made me lose  faith in Cannonical’s abiltiy to deliver a useful distribution for  serious computer users.  Sic tranist gloria Ubuntu…it was -really- good  there for awhile.

---

### Post by Hated On Mostly on 2011-10-14
> **Roasted said:**
> You do realize you can install another desktop environment... right?

> **wolfen69 said:**
> I guess some people don't realize that you can use *any* DE in ubuntu, and soon, *every* distro will drop support for gnome 2. And, those people that can't live without gnome 2 will have to quit using computers. Whatever will we do without it? Oh the horror!


I have a feeling you have never tried this and then used the system fulltime...

Yes, one can do this but it never works smoothly. You lose functionality that a default distribution install provides.

ex:

Default Ubuntu install = works

Default Xubuntu install = works

Default Ubuntu install + xfce4 added manually = kind of works, but lots of things don't work correctly

Default Xubuntu install + gnome-shell added manually = kind of works, but lots of things don't work properly

more specific example off the top of my head: You can't configure your sound card anymore when you boot into Ubuntu using xfce whereas Ubuntu default configures it just fine.

Not to mention most non-advanced Linux/Ubuntu users will probably end up installing the full Xubuntu desktop rather than just xfce which will cause even more problems and confusion for them.


> **cariboo907 said:**
> To me, this is a disturbing trend in Ubuntu users, that if it isn't installed by default, they won't use it.

Believe it or not a lot of people just want to use their computers to get things done rather than learning every inch of it and tweaking everything. Even though I could nagivate and eventually figure out solutions to every problem caused due to tweaking Ubuntu, I don't want to. I want to install, change a few settings very quickly, and get back to being productive.


Here is a perfect comment about why the forced changed to Unity is bad...

> 
Unity fails the mom test specifically because they hide the menus. My mom can't figure out how to do anything on a computer if it's not visible on the screen at any given moment.

So if she wants to print something, she thinks "Hmm. Usually I go to a menu. But there is no menu, so I cannot print." I tell her that she should just remember that there MAY be a secret menu up there if she mouses up, but she does not remember that because she is not good with computers. So she runs classic Gnome 2 and will probably never run anything past 11.04.

This is the kind of stupid thing that makes me embarrassed because I'm the one that told her Ubuntu was easy enough for her to use. Did no one try seeing if computer illiterates could do things like print with Unity? I find it hard to believe that any kind of study wouldn't have instantly revealed that hiding the menus causes problems when less savvy users try to do anything that's not immediately visible on the window.

### Post by kohoutek1 on 2011-10-14
More elegant, integrated, self-sufficient Unity under 11.10 than under 11.04.

Far less configurable, though.

On the other hand, I've just about made the launcher disappear, thanks to a couple of new settings in the Compiz Unity Plugin, so that it only shows when I engage Expo or Scale. 

I'm looking for two more changes, then I'll be happy with Unity:

1) A way to theme the menubar with a Graphite color
2) A way to get the Dash to launch from my AWN, which is at the bottom.

BTW, I didn't like seeing my name in the menubar, so I just deleted it in Users/Groups and replaced it with one tap of the spacebar. Now, no name in the menubar.

"Sounds like Mac..." Yep. That's where I migrated from.

### Post by jejones3141 on 2011-10-14
Upgraded to 11.10; it went smoothly.

I am not a happy camper--I have a wife, sister, niece, and a couple of good friends who I've set up with Ubuntu and who have become used to GNOME 2. They're not hard core computer users--and now they have no choice but to learn a different window manager/windowing environment.

The seemingly gratuitous differences between "fallback" mode and GNOME 2 are going to irritate them greatly. *Alt* right click?! Why can't I move my applets, or that clock in the middle of the panel? Where did the sensors applet go? Can't I restart my computer any more? All I see is shut down.

If there's any tutorial to help people move from GNOME 2 to GNOME 3, I haven't seen it. For that matter, I get to explain the new gdm to them, too. (BTW, is it configurable to require typing the user name? I hope so; Mr. McCann was so concerned about the catastrophic business effects of letting the user configure the screensaver, I'd hope he'd be at least as concerned about the security issue of J. Random Social Engineer seeing a list of user names on a momentarily unattended monitor.)

I personally will give fallback mode a shot, but I despise GNOME 3 and the attitude of its designers, and didn't care for Unity. I'm the sort who'll spend some time getting e17, KDE, or fluxbox configured as I like, but the friends and relatives we have all encouraged to use Linux may not be--how many will get fed up and hear Microsoft's siren song? Why make such a huge break with what they're used to?

### Post by Flymo on 2011-10-14
Downloading ISOs now....

Determined to try the latest Ubuntu, but had to wipe Natty since it was so flaky on our hardware, as well as endlessly annoying to use, for us.  

It's not alone - we find the Gnome Shell thing almost as hard to use.  Both of them feel subtly obstructive, and (as an earlier poster pointed out) Unity fails the Mom test.  

Hidden user access icons - like 'Main Menu' is fun for some, like finding the Easter Eggs in a game, maybe.  But I'm not adapting to that well, despite trying hard with Natty.  What my friends say is unprintable, and I'm just trying to be polite.  

In a spirit of exploration I'm genuinely trying to get the hang of this UnityGnomeShell idea, but getting nowhere.  Fear that it will end up in my KDE4 box - the only KDE4 configurations that I get on with (after years of trying) are ones that most closely resemble KDE3.  But they use huge resources on our ancient hardware and get wiped.

We'll try our Oneiric ISOs live, might even install one.  But if they are no better than the Beta 2 we'll be wiping our 'testing' partitions again and trying something else.

This comes to you from an ancient Compaq C300 with a snail-like Celeron 420 that feels young, fresh and vigorous again under Bodhi Linux 1.2.0

The surprising (and disappointing for Ubuntu) thing for us is that Bodhi is not only prettier (with E17 compositing) using far less RAM, but is substantially more stable than Natty.  But then, it's based on Lucid, Ubuntu's best release to date for us.

Be nice to see Unity sorted out and Ubuntu back on track. <sigh>

Here goes with the Oneiric CD burning, I see the downloads are done!

Wish us luck.....:guitar:

### Post by koleoptero on 2011-10-14
My 2 cents:

Unity seems to be working far better than in 11.04 so congrats on that. I can't use it, and actually hate it, but it's a personal preference thing. The fact that half the apps are writen in gtk3 and the other half in gtk2 is a nightmare. The lack of customizability by default is also vastly disappointing. The slower boot times and such stuff I find irrelevant. Overall it's a nice usable thing but not for me.

Xubuntu on the other hand was a relief when I tried it. It has come a long way, has a nice selection of apps, and doesn't disappoint when it comes to customizability. It certainly is a better option for me.

Kubuntu also works flawlessly now for me, even before I installed the low fat settings package. I think I might stick to that for a while since it's a nice DE with amazing applications.

I haven't tried gnome-shell but with my lousy graphics card I don't think that's an option. Plus from what I've read of the way it manages tasks and desktops it's not what I'd prefer.

All complaints aside congratulations to everyone for a release that's a vast improvement over 11.04 on all fields.

### Post by viperdvman on 2011-10-14
Well, I've downloaded Oneric for both [COLOR="DarkOrange"]Ubuntu[/COLOR] and [COLOR="Blue"]Kubuntu[/COLOR]. Right now, I'm playing with Kubuntu 11.10 since I already had Kubuntu 11.04 on a USB drive. The little restore button on the application control widget doesn't work in 11.10 like it did in 11.04. But overall, I like how I'm able to switch activities now with a sleeker-looking icon instead of a taskbar-like interface that can take up a whole panel if you're one of those KDE users who uses activities. I don't like having more than 1 panel in any DE (Mac OS X type docks aside), and don't want to be forced to make a second panel. That's why I like 11.10's activities better than 11.04's.

Overall, aside from restore not working right in Netbook mode, I like Kubuntu 11.10, and it's already replaced my Kubuntu LiveUSB. For now, I'll keep it a LiveUSB. I'll decide later if I really want to install Kubuntu alogside my primary Ubuntu (Natty) and soon Ubuntu 11.10. I have KDE Plasma installed on my Ubuntu Natty, but it's not the complete package like Kubuntu's is.

### Post by jejones3141 on 2011-10-14
> **twozeronine said:**
> ....I just installed an icon pack and had to install another program (dconf) in order to edit a setting to use it. Why make us jump through hoops like that?

Because the people behind GNOME 3 would rather you didn't customize it. From an interview with Jon McCann: "And I think there is a lot of value to have that experience you show the world to be consistent. In GNOME2 we didn't do that particularly well because everyone's desktop was different."

### Post by cariboo on 2011-10-14
@neiljansons open an terminal and type:

```
unity --reset-icons
```

that should restore your icons. Be warned that any other icons you have in the launcher may be removed.

### Post by Dangertux on 2011-10-15
I really like 11.10, I think to be honest too many people are focusing on Unity (as always). Unity is NOT 11.10, just one part of it.  However, it's one of the least  critical parts in my opinion. 

Things I like about 11.10

- Access to new Ruby libraries FINALLY
- Return of native Xen support in the kernel
- Kernel 3.0 for that matter.
- Open Source Radeon drivers actually work WELL. 
- For those who didn't upgrade from source or ppa, Apache 2.20 (got really tired of hearing about CVE 2011-3192)
- The new Unity dash looks REALLY good.


Things I am indifferent to in 11.10

- Unity in general, minus the dash, I love that.
- Little bit heavier on resources.


Things I dislike in 11.10

- Unity hatred from everyone.
- Runs a little hot. 

In my opinion great release :-)

### Post by neu5eeCh on 2011-10-15
Ubuntu, I'm just not persuaded by Unity.

Neither Unity nor Gnome3 have demonstrated that their systems (the Icon UI) are more efficient, more usable, or an advance over the simple drop-down menu. Instead, we have ever more lenses, filters, and search trees to accomplish what can done with a drop-down menu in a fraction of the time. Beyond that, I like to customize my system - Gnome3 is particularly resistant. As [Jejones wrote]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=11343734&postcount=129"):

"...the people behind GNOME 3 would rather you didn't customize it.  From an interview with Jon McCann: "And I think there is a lot of value  to have that experience you show the world to be consistent. In GNOME2  we didn't do that particularly well because everyone's desktop was  different."

Well, sorry Jon, your consistency is *my* inefficiency. Unity's customization amounts to little more than window dressing. I've been using XFCE since Unity was introduced and will continue to use it. It's also what I install for users new to Linux. However, I'm game to try the latest Kubuntu - looking forward to it. 

A review at [Linux User]("http://www.linuxuser.co.uk/reviews/ubuntu-11-10-review-oneiric-ocelot-beautiful-but-deadly/") reflects my own outlook.

### Post by aaaantoine on 2011-10-15
I had been annoyed with KDE because Kwin ran so relatively slow on my laptop compared to Ubuntu with Compiz.

Now, in 11.10, not only does it seem to run slower, but there is also lag when moving the windows around.

Also, at some point during my previous session, it stopped taking input from my touchpad until I restarted.

Thirdly, the fonts have increased dramatically in size, and I can't figure out how to fix them.

Fourth, I am disappointed that the hot-corner for the hidden Unity launcher has gone away (though I understand sacrifices have to be made when moving around UI elements).

---

### Post by Larkspur on 2011-10-15
> **aaaantoine said:**
> Thirdly, the fonts have increased dramatically in size, and I can't figure out how to fix them.

Fourth, I am disappointed that the hot-corner for the hidden Unity launcher has gone away (though I understand sacrifices have to be made when moving around UI elements).

For the fonts, install gnome-tweak-tool from the Software Centre.  Regarding the hot-corner, it's still there, but the method to use it has changed: move the mouse right into the left-hand corner, then move down, keeping the pointer against the screen edge.  I think it's been changed because users were complaining about invoking the launcher when they were trying to use controls in e.g. full-screen Firefox.

---

### Post by aaaantoine on 2011-10-15
> **Larkspur said:**
> For the fonts, install gnome-tweak-tool from the Software Centre.  Regarding the hot-corner, it's still there, but the method to use it has changed: move the mouse right into the left-hand corner, then move down, keeping the pointer against the screen edge.  I think it's been changed because users were complaining about invoking the launcher when they were trying to use controls in e.g. full-screen Firefox.

Thanks for the tip about gnome-tweak-tool.  That at least solves one problem for me. :)

I don't think the hot-corner as you describe it behaves any differently from just putting the mouse to the left edge of the screen.

### Post by Algus on 2011-10-15
Been using it for a couple days now...

Now mind you, I've done the exact opposite of what people say to do, and I don't think I've had a clean install of Ubunu since 8.10 or 9.04.  I just use the upgrade tool every time.  Because of that I sometimes have some...strangeness...

Ubuntu 11.04 never really worked right for me, especially in Unity.  My webcam worked maybe half the time and I had major shutdown issues.   One of my other experiences has been having to spend two or three days bugfixing, partly because of not doing a clean install.   

I've had NONE of those issues with 11.10.  The upgrade tool seemed relatively quick by upgrade tool standards and I've had no bugs to speak of.  My webcam works.  My internet works.   Startup and shutdown seem faster and better (even though startup times are allegedly longer now).  My laptop doesn't seem to be running as hot either.  

The one thing I did need to take care of was bringing back screensavers but that was a super easy fix and a virtual nonissue.   So by and large I've been very happy.

I also enjoy the further online integration features.  Ubuntu fetching Facebook updates and such for me is a nifty little feature.  I think some of this stuff existed before but for the most part it seems much easier to use now.

**The New Coke (Unity)**


"The Coca-Cola Company introduced reformulated Coca-Cola®, often referred to as "new Coke®," marking the first formula change in 99 years. The company didn't set out to create the firestorm of consumer protest that ensued; instead, The Coca-Cola Company intended to re-energize its Coca-Cola brand and the cola category in its largest market, the United States.

That firestorm ended with the return of the original formula, now called Coca-Cola classic®, a few months later. The return of original formula Coca-Cola on July 11, 1985, put the cap on 79 days that revolutionized the soft-drink industry, transformed The Coca-Cola Company and stands today as testimony to the power of taking intelligent risks, even when they don't quite work as intended."

### Post by jmmeyer on 2011-10-15
I came back to linux after a few years hiatus, by installing 11.04 on my HP mini netbook. I was very impressed at how much nicer it ran than the stock WinXP it came with. This is kind of a play-toy machine, so I did just that - I tweaked and fiddled and tried out different packages and generally had fun with it. Unity was "different," but worked well on the tiny, wide screen. It was a little "unfinished" feeling, though.

I read plenty of articles about how much improvement had been made in the new release, so I chose "upgrade" from the update manager. After the reboot, everything seemed to be ok. Then I found nautilus was crashing whenever I tried to open a folder. Found the fix (remove open-terminal) and then it was ok. Then tried to adjust screen brightness, and the slider was spastic, going from full bright to very dim repeatedly as I moved it. Not a big deal, others had logged the same bug; I could wait for a fix. 

I ran firefox with no problem, then I minimized it. The firefox menu stated up at the top. I opened a terminal, but the menu was still firefox. I closed them both, and unity crashed, leaving a blank desktop with my pointer. Tried to get to a console with function keys but couldn't. I finally hit ctl-alt-del to prompt "logout" so I did. Logged back in, and still only a blank desktop. 

Rebooted, same problem. Hmmm. I'm not really a power user, so I really didn't know where to start fixing this. So I downloaded 11.10 and made a pendrive. 

During install, I told it to wipe and install. Figured I had troubles from the existing 11.04 that were not cleared away by the upgrade. I didn't really have any files to protect, since I only used this for web stuff and playing with the os.

I let the install cook and watched a movie. At the end of the install, the screen had blanked, and I couldn't get it to come back with trackpad or keys. I hit "enter" and it rebooted, and came up with 11.10 running. 

Things looked ok, so I tried installing the samba conf tool. Software center claimed there was an error, but seemed to complete. When that didn't run, I found online that a dependency for a library was not included in the package, so I added the library and the configuration tool started working.

I installed a couple other packages, and each time, the software center claimed an error but the install seemed to work. 

I switched to my main laptop for a while to research this package issue, and when I came back to the mini, it had blanked the screen, and nothing I could do would wake it up.

I really liked the improvements made to unity and loved that the battery and CPU monitor indicators really worked.  So I was reluctant to revert back to 11.04, but I did, just because I was tired of major issues that I was too ignorant to fix.

I realize now that casual users who know a little but not a lot about how to fix linux problems should probably not jump on a new release on the second day. I also realize I should always try the live USB before committing to a new release.

I'll wait a couple months and keep an eye on the forums and launchpad, then try 11.10 again from a USB stick.

---

### Post by Paulgirardin on 2011-10-15
I decided to check-out Gnome shell.

### Post by lovinglinux on 2011-10-16
I was using Kubuntu for the past 2 or 3 releases, if I remember correctly. I love KDE, but I decided to give Unity a try with the release of Ocelot. 

I did a fresh install. Initially, I had problems with Compiz and decided to wipe my config files. Recently I discovered that wasn't really necessary. Just had to delete ~/.config/compiz-1/compizconfig/config file.

Anyway, except for Compiz, which crashes depending on which plugin you mess with, I am really enjoying Oneiric. Everything is working out-of-the-box so far, including RecordMyDesktop that was broken in the past two releases.

I like the simplicity of Unity, the interface is very polished and some of the new default applications, like Déjà Dup Backup are really awesome. In my opinion, this is a great release and I will probably return to Ubuntu because of it.

### Post by kwildman on 2011-10-16
I started with Ubuntu around Intrepid and been on board as my primary OS since then.  I am an IT Director in a windows shop so I really dont like having to deal with the same issues on my hardware.   I have 3 computers that i routinely use throughout the day all running Ubuntu.  I also have another 3 laptops that my teenage kids use.  

I really like unity and the direction that ubuntu is heading.  Unity is an easy and intuitive interface.  My kids had no problems adapting.  I have let their grandparents borrow laptops and they get along just fine.  

I find it odd that the majority of negative comments come from seasoned linux users.  the people with a couple of whacks of the keyboard that could be running lxde, kde, flux, etc.  The goal of a distro should not be to make it exclusive for linux experts but rather make it accessible, clean, pretty, and fun for NEW USERs.   If we want to own the desktop then we need to win the hearts and minds of the TYPICAL users.   TYPICAL users dont know anything about computers - if they did then there would be no Geeksquad or [www.fixmypc.com](www.fixmypc.com) type sites.  

I use the command line for about 90% of my work.   The other 10% is for email, web, etc. and the unity interface is as good as anything.

### Post by viperdvman on 2011-10-16
Well, I've played with [COLOR=Blue]**Kubuntu 11.10**[/COLOR] plenty, and plan on keeping it around on a persistent LiveUSB for a while. It really isn't much different from Kubuntu 11.04, to be honest, outside of a few enhancements to some minor stuff, and appearence enhancements. That low-fat settings might be useful for older computers that want to use KDE or for better performance.

I'll be playing with [COLOR=DarkOrange]**Ubuntu 11.10**[/COLOR] pretty soon on a LiveUSB. I've done a quick trial once already on my desktop, and it doesn't seem play well with my desktop *(no GNOME 3-based distro has so far... it's worked, but had performance issues)*, but works great on my netbook. I'm guessing my completely different buses between my 2 NVidia adapters is the culprit *(one onboard 7050/630i, the other PCIe 6800GT... I've heard that setup doesn't play well with Linux)*, so replace both with a single and much faster dual-head unit, and that should fix the problem... and might even make my dual displays work in Linux.

Anyhow, I'll be trying it probably exclusively on the netbook as a LiveUSB for a while before I attempt to try it with my desktop. I'll still do the full install in November as they are always more "tweakable" than a LiveUSB session. I'll keep you posted on my progress with Oneric Ubuntu, as well as anything I like/dislike about Oneric Kubuntu.

As for those who don't like Unity, and want to leave Ubuntu altogether because of it, I highly recommend at least trying a few of the other **official** derivatives first. There's Kubuntu, Xubuntu, and Lubuntu that have the more classic "Windows-like" desktop. There are other Ubuntu-based distros out there as well still using GNOME 2.x for now, [COLOR=SeaGreen]Linux Mint[/COLOR] being the most popular (second most popular Linux distro too). Give the other derivatives a chance. At least try to stay in the Ubuntu family before you decide to search for another distro to settle into. That's the beauty of Ubuntu, and of Linux... we have choices. :)

### Post by Pcmechanic on 2011-10-16
Installed 11.10 on a Dell studio laptop (fresh install as the upgrade just fouled everything up). Here's what I've found so far (for and against)

For :

Its fast - quite a noticeable improvement in performance

I can install gnome shell :D

Less than 6 gig for a full operating system that's tweaked exactly how I want it in under 3 hours ? - Impressed !

Against :

Unity is a pain in the bum and a hindrance. It slows down workflow

Major problem with Firefox 7 causing system to hang or crash. Resolved by dumping Firefox and moving to Chromium

Update just fouled everything up and system wouldn't boot into Ubuntu. Clean install and everything's fine

### Post by MonolithImmortal on 2011-10-16
Dear everyone who claims that not liking unity = get off the grass mentality,

Disliking unity != being a dinosaur, being stuck in the past, or unable to adapt.

I however dislike unity. It's broken in several ways, is counter-intuitive in others, is extremely uncustomizable, and does not work for my workflow at all. In short, it is everything that I don't want.

Banshee crashes all the time, the version of unity is worse than the last, battery life is terrible (hint, I installed Jupiter and went from 3 hour battery life to 7 hour battery life), had xorg crash a few times and a few other smaller things.

### Post by marcin_ant on 2011-10-16
I upgraded to Oneiric on laptop and because of some strange bug with dpkg I gave up and reinstalled on workstation.

And now I'm very, very sad. I'm Ubuntu user from 2005 and I have to say that this so called "release" is worst Ubuntu I have ever seen.

1. On my Vertex SSD Oneiric boots slower than Natty (fresh install).

2. There is no easy way to install fully functional Java and Java plugin in browser (I know that it's because of Oracle, but... this is very important feature).

3. Unity... no words. I got used to launchers, I even like to work in full screen mode (with emacs ;-) ), but I really don't understand why they think that I don't need and don't want to know how many windows of the same application I opened?

4. but there is more bad news:

There is NO EASY way to change default FONT!!!

I had to install and tweak some entries with gconf-editor to change default monospace font!!!

There is also no way to disable screen dimming on laptop when idle!

I don't know how to install new desktop themes. There is no easy and intuitive way to get themes and install them.

Settings for default applications moved to system INFO icon. Why?

If this icon was useless than why there are two icons for keyboard settings? I got "keyboard" and "keyboard layout". I also have two separate icons for screen settings. WHY???

Then Software Center... Synaptic removed and what instead? Mess...

Software Center list is unsorted in language different than english.
It is so annoying that first thing I had to do was apt-get install synaptic.

Come on! New release should be better - not worse!!! How could you release so buggy and unfinished product? I just don't understand. I'm very dissapointed and just sad because I did a lot to promote Ubuntu in my neighbourhood, customers. Now I just cannot advertise this "something".

Linux is system for power users. It should be easy and user friendly but it shouldn't be "Ubuntu for dummies"! Ubuntu and Linux is loved because it's easily customizable, powerfull etc. Now you just want to cut these users out and try to amaze OSX fans. But in my opinion it's just suicide.

---

### Post by Kozmyk on 2011-10-16
My laptop prefers Natty (Lenovo T61)
I don't like Unity at all.

This new version did me a favour in pushing me to find another distro.
I've found a new home at Mint. 
In Mint my Ext VGA works and my DVDs will play.
I can now get back to doing what I do instead of fighting my PC.

I found this YUMI app a very useful way to shop around alternative distros by running a stack of them from a USB stick instead of messing around with multi-booting.

[http://www.pendrivelinux.com/yumi-multiboot-usb-creator/](http://www.pendrivelinux.com/yumi-multiboot-usb-creator/) 

I've kept 11.04 Natty on my HDD on a much shrunken partition, just in case things improve in the future. ;-)

### Post by lovinglinux on 2011-10-16
> **marcin_ant said:**
> 1. On my Vertex SSD Oneiric boots slower than Natty (fresh install).

I haven't noticed any difference.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> 2. There is no easy way to install fully functional Java and Java plugin in browser (I know that it's because of Oracle, but... this is very important feature).

I am running Java applications and Java plugin just fine. Just install restricted-extras.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> 3. Unity... no words. I got used to launchers, I even like to work in full screen mode (with emacs ;-) ), but I really don't understand why they think that I don't need and don't want to know how many windows of the same application I opened?

Just look the the launcher closely. For each window, there is a small white thingy on the left side of the launcher. When you click it, the windows are shown by expo compiz plugin, so you can pick the one you want. For me, this is better than having multiple window buttons in the panel. A lot cleaner and efficient.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> 4. but there is more bad news:

There is NO EASY way to change default FONT!!!

I had to install and tweak some entries with gconf-editor to change default monospace font!!!

It is very easy to do that with Gnome Tweak Tool, also known as Advanced Settings.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> There is also no way to disable screen dimming on laptop when idle!

Haven't tried that yet, because I am using a desktop.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> I don't know how to install new desktop themes. There is no easy and intuitive way to get themes and install them.

There are somes themes out there to install as deb. They become available in the list. If not, you can also change themes with Gnome Tweak Tool.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> Settings for default applications moved to system INFO icon. Why?

If this icon was useless than why there are two icons for keyboard settings? I got "keyboard" and "keyboard layout". I also have two separate icons for screen settings. WHY???

I don't know and don't really care. I was able to find those settings pretty quick and it was very simple to use.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> Then Software Center... Synaptic removed and what instead? Mess...

Software Center list is unsorted in language different than english.
It is so annoying that first thing I had to do was apt-get install synaptic.

This is the first time I am actually using Software Center. It has evolved a lot and it is a lot better.

I also install Synaptic, which takes less than 30 seconds, but I am really enjoying the Software Center when looking for new applications. The Dash integration is also very nice.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> Come on! New release should be better - not worse!!! How could you release so buggy and unfinished product? I just don't understand. I'm very dissapointed and just sad because I did a lot to promote Ubuntu in my neighbourhood, customers. Now I just cannot advertise this "something".

Linux is system for power users. It should be easy and user friendly but it shouldn't be "Ubuntu for dummies"! Ubuntu and Linux is loved because it's easily customizable, powerfull etc. Now you just want to cut these users out and try to amaze OSX fans. But in my opinion it's just suicide.

Ubuntu should be easy to use by everyone, not just "power users", otherwise it wont gain market share. If you are a "power user" and you are not happy, you can install Gnome Shell, Gnome Classic or use Kununtu, Xubuntu, Lubuntu...

I am a "power user", coming from KDE, which I used for the last 2 or 3 releases. I was a Gnome user since Hardy. I am very satisfied with Oneiric and probably won't go back to KDE. I love the simplicity and everything works out-of-the-box for me.

### Post by Olph on 2011-10-16
It took me most of the weekend and the help of friends to recover a reasonable facsimile of my previous Gnome desktop.

I understand the popularity of the Smart Phone Look on a PC, but I don't like having it imposed upon me.

With 11.04 I just had to select Gnome Classic desktop and I continued with life.

I would like to have had that option with 11.10.

Interestingly, just before this upgrade, I told a Systems Designer friend I now had the best computer I had seen or used in the past 25 years.

If some people like the Smart Phone look and function to their PC, more power to them.

However, it shouldn't require that much work to keep what you've got.

I plan on waiting on the next upgrade to see what Ubuntu does, as far as leaving user options open and easy.

If Ubuntu imposes a look and function on me again, without an easy way back to my own setup, I may switch to a different distribution.

### Post by Olph on 2011-10-17
Yes, we all resist change.

However, up until 11.10 the changes corresponded to improvements, and a user could easily pick the flavor of Ubuntu he wanted.

I don't mind doing a little work for an upgrade, but this version represented too much work and suggests a trend towards one desktop serves all.

The Smart Phone Look works for Smart Phones because they have limited screen space and thus fewer options. This means the designer must dictate the appearance of the Smart Phone desktop.

However, with a PC and most laptops we have a lot more space and many options that work much better than a Smart Phone.

Yes, a person can adapt.

However, in my mind, we sometimes stand to lose by adapting, and this looks like a trend...a bad trend.

---

### Post by westie457 on 2011-10-17
No rant against Unity, feelings are ambivalent, when it becomes the the fall-back option to whatever comes in the future I will use it for most of the things I do with a computer. Unity is not good or bad, just different and it works. Just as Linux is different to Windows, it is a learning curve. Most of us here have had similar learning curves to adjust from one OS to another. 

The way I upgraded to Oneiric did not cause any issues. Apart from the usual wireless issues, used the same drivers as in Natty. It went like this.

Running Maverick upgraded to Natty then downloaded the Oneiric ISO burned to USB. Installed straight over the top of Natty w/out formatting partitions. The ISO was a daily-build of Beta 2 downloaded and installed 2 days before official release.

### Post by lovinglinux on 2011-10-17
> **marcin_ant said:**
> Ok. Let's try. Software Center -> Search "restriced-extras" 

1. Polish translation: "No items in Internet -> Wszystkie match "restricted-extras" "

The only translated word is "Wszystkie" !!!

2. Couldn't find specific package in Software Center? Pathetic...

3. But thank you. It works now (I hope that my bank will understand that icedtea=java. I will try that later).


You need to enable Multiverse repository in the Software Sources.

[[IMG]http://www2.picturepush.com/photo/a/6771515/220/6771515.png[/IMG]](http://picturepush.com/public/6771515)

> **marcin_ant said:**
> 1. This "small white thingy" is visible only when launcher is visible. When application is maximized it's hidden.

You can configure the launcher to be visible all the time through *Compiz Config Settings Manager*. Look for the Unity plugin there.

Also see [http://castrojo.tumblr.com/post/4795149014/the-power-users-guide-to-unity](http://castrojo.tumblr.com/post/4795149014/the-power-users-guide-to-unity)

> **marcin_ant said:**
> 2. On sreen with big resolution (I got 1920 horizontally) there is a lot of wasted space on top panel. I just don't understant why there is no window indicator there.

Window indicators can take a lot of space if you open many windows, which would probably cause issues with global menu.

There are solutions tho. You can for instance install a simple panel that will do the job. I don't remember the name of it, but you probably can find it on OMG Ubuntu or WEBUPD8.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> I don't want to google and install additional unsupported software just to change font or theme! Don't you remember that Ubuntu motto was that it "just works"?


> **marcin_ant said:**
> Again - I DON'T want additional software. I just want things to be there.

It works, out-of-the-box, is not their fault if you don't like the default. Installing *Advanced Settings* or any additional application shouldn't be a big deal for experienced users who like to customize their desktop.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> Believe me - it's not there. It's so annoying. I read something in web browser, my laptop is connected to AC and I need to move mouse every few seconds because Ubuntu saves energy for me... Sooooo annoying!

I don't have a notebook, but I am pretty sure you can change that behavior from "System Settings >>> Screen".

> **marcin_ant said:**
> 

I looks better. It looks like Android market or something. Like e-commerce. But it's not usefull at all. 

I know that it should be easy but not too easy and annoying. I cannot believe that someone released package that shows unsorted list of software  while it's not in english? In 2011? We had problems with translations and sorting in 2004. Linux was and is multilingual.

Have you clicked the drop-down menu on the right to sort the applications by *Name*, *Relevance* and so on? If that doesn't work, you could contribute and report it so it can be fixed. 

> **marcin_ant said:**
> If you are power user - you know that there is a thing called Synaptic. And you can be surprised  that it's not installed by default.

If you are new to Ubuntu - you don't know such thing. And you are stuck with Software Center mess.

As I said in my previous post, I always install Synaptic after installing Ubuntu. Not a big deal. The developers cannot put the entire repository in the release, because it must fit a 700 Mb CD. It makes sense to not include Synpatic, since newcomers don't really need it and power users can install Synaptic themselves in less than 30 seconds.

Software Center works like a charm, is intuitive and has lots of useful info.

> **marcin_ant said:**
> Well, I'm not. I'm really dissapointed. And I think that it won't gain market share. Maybe it will on tablets or netbooks but on desktops... not this way.

Well, you can install Gnome Shell and use it or use Gnome Classic. Have you tried those? You can also use KDE, which is very customizable.

Flash even works better.  But a few things confuse me.  The default email client is now Thunderbird, but the drop down menu (the mail icon in the systray) still has evolution in it. ### Post by JayKay3OOO on 2011-10-17
Having used the Gnome versions in the past I like this upgrade to unity a lot.

Unity has been polished to a point where I could conceivably use it over Windows 7. The tweaks are small, but noticeable. If anything it's just easier and quicker to find your applications now.

This version certain does feel more like a desktop operating system than a mobile OS that happens to be for a desktop environment and the tweaks make it easier and more efficient to use with a traditional mouse/touch pad & keyboard interface.

There certainly is more room for improvement and I'm really looking forward to what comes up in the next version. Like many I wish progress was faster, but progress seems to be going in a direction that I agree with.

I still find having to manually set-up libdvdread4 a kludge. It would be nice to be able to play DVD films (encrypted) without having to do anything,

The only reason I have to keep windows 7 is because the current set of AMD drivers for the E350 zacate AMD fusion chip make the whole experience a bit laggy. Pointless when the main use of this system is for Video.

Works fine on my 4 core machine though.

Good job overall and thanks for all your time and effort for the continued development of Ubuntu.

---

### Post by seatex on 2011-10-17
I don't think people are hating just to hate 11.10.  Many, like myself, have been using Ubuntu for many years now, and it has been the most popular distro for quite a while - many consider Ubuntu the "face of linux" due to it's popularity and mindshare among those considering linux.

I think a lot of people are concerned and frustrated with choices being made by Canonical targeted at attracting people who don't know how to use a computer and are drawn to a simple (dumbed down) yet pretty interface.

However, this new interface is less intuitive and efficient while also limiting choices in customization and multi-tasking ease.  People don't like feeling that developers don't care about their concerns - only their vision of what they want users to adapt to.

So, personally, I am criticizing more than hating, and hoping the critiques help Ubuntu get back on the right track.

### Post by marcin_ant on 2011-10-17
> **tumbes2000 said:**
> Man the negativity is crazy!  I am getting the feeling that some people do not want Ubuntu to be a mainstream OS.  After my "conversion" in May of this year I tell everyone about Ubuntu and how great of an OS I think it is.  Yet it is disappointing to see the community attack the latest release with such hate.  If you are a fan of another distro great, but why come on the broads and bash Unity?  The stated mission of Ubuntu is to expand the user base and to do that the OS has to be easy to use for the typical user not someone that is going to spend two weeks tweeking their system.  If that's what you want, no problem there are plenty of options out there for that, but why the hate?  Does someone mail you cookies after you declare to the world you hate Unity?

You converted to Ubuntu in May this year. I converted June... 2005.

So, please don't tell me that I hate Ubuntu, and I don't want it to be mainstream.

I just hate bugs. Lack of quality managment. I hate wasting time and resources on changes not accepted by community while there are tons of bugs and problems in mainstream packages.

Community is the only power that Ubuntu has. Without it - it will die and will be nothing. Canonical is not Microsoft. They do not own code and they are not main developer.

I understand that Canonical wants to gain market share and they need bigger user base that finally could convert to customers.

But believe me or not - but there is no something like "typical user".

Linux as a product has to have target. You need to know why people would want to use this and what kind of users will newer switch to linux.

First thing - you need to forget about gamers.

You got nothing for them.

Second - graphics designers, photo editors etc. - users addicted to Adobe producs and mainly Apple lovers.

Than you need to forget about business. No one will switch to Ubuntu while software they really need is available only for Windows (MS Office, accounting, billing, invoice etc. )

Lack of integration with mobile devices such as Blackberry is another reason.

I know that you can say that Ubuntu has everything they need. But saddly it's not true. 

So. Than you have something you call "typical user" (propably). Users that have not so sophisticated requirements.

They need web browser, mail client, few simple applications to edit and share their media - photos, family video etc.

Ok. Ubuntu is pretty enough for them since 2008. They should be happy.

But believe me - they will not contribute. They will not develop gnome. They will not send patches.

They will complain that their printer doesn't work or there is no way to synchronize their cell phone with evolution.. ekhm Thunderbird in Oneiric.

And we also have so called "power users".
They are active and usefull contributors. They send bug reports and... patches. They have specific requirements and in my opinion Ubuntu absolutely should not cut them off!

Ubuntu is yet another DISTRIBUTION. Distributor should select valuable software from upstream and provide this software as nice box collection.

That should be important base. But let's see. From my individual point of view.

I want to use Ubuntu to develop web applications.

I use Compass. [http://compass-style.org/]("http://compass-style.org/")

Ubuntu package is libcompass-0.10.6. As I can see in mainstream repo it was uploaded October 27, 2010

Current version is 0.11.5 uploaded July 11, 2011

So in "new" Oneiric I get version that is one year old.

Let's see most popular javasript library: jQuery.

Ubuntu version 1.6.2, upstream 1.6.4.

Than haml [http://haml-lang.com]("http://haml-lang.com")

Ubuntu version 3.0.25 uploaded December 17, 2010, upstream 3.1.3 - another almost year old package.

Php 5 - upgraded TODAY to 5.3.6 - upstream release 5.3.8

Redmine - Ubuntu version 1.1.3 from 2011.04.29 while next release was 2011.05.30 (Debian import freeze was June 30th).

I don't have time to check all packages important for me. But as you can see I cannot be very happy with this new, fresh release?

I understand that Canonical want to improve desktop (in their opinion) but in my opinion they shouldn't abandon their basic job.

### Post by xtremo on 2011-10-18

However, what sealed it's fate was the sidebar continually disappearing and re-appearing for no logical reason. Then Firefox and Thunderbird started hanging and freezing....which has never happened before.

I'm a web developer and PC's are a tool for me to do my job....I just haven't got the time to find workarounds for this stuff. So Ubuntu came off and Xubuntu went on instead. Now I can get back to work.

I hate to say it, but I think Unity is not a viable solution for me. It may work well for others, and I'm sure some will love the bling factor. But for me as a tool to do my job, it's no use whatsoever.

### Post by JayKay3OOO on 2011-10-18
Kittens may die, but there are always new ones to replace them.

I'd like raise my hand (just imagine that) and say that as customisable as gnome 2 was it made you waste a lot of time getting it the way you wanted. It looks so old and outdated just as web pages that use a lot of web 2.0 content do.

Gnome 2.0 looks familiar, just like mom & apple pie do, but these things don't exactly excite.

It's hardly an attractive alternative to OSX or Windows 7. It looked better than XP, but XP had 10 years of no core upgrades.

Gnome 2 can be made to look swish, but to get new users on board you need a more swish looking interface out of the box.

The more I fiddled with docs, themes & transparencies the more I found the whole Gnome 2.0 experience usable, but bland. I was infact trying to make it something it was not.

Just like I think 'how cool would it to be to use Windows XP' & then I remember how rubbish it looks. Belive me, I had a sentimental boner over windows 98 for a bit. I fired up the disk in a VM and got all excited and then opened it ready for the wonderful 98ness and yes, it sucked. Like opening the best looking present under the tree and realising it's just a pair of socks in a big shiny box and that you only imagined something amazing would be inside.

Using an OS is about the experience. You have to like the aesthetics of an OS because you end up spending way more time inside it than you should.

The hate on unity is like the hate I had for XP. As humans we simply don't like change.

The internet gives those people that hate change the most a voice and get remembered the most. The kinds of people that are still locked in the 90's and refused to listen to anything but rock and roll in the event that they get tainted by other influences.

---

### Post by psynophile on 2011-10-19
Disappointing.

I've been a *nix user since 1996. I've used every distribution from Gentoo to Arch at one time or another. I often read, modify and submit code for F/OSS projects. I've used both KDE (since version 1) and Gnome (even before it was a "window manager").

I switched to Ubuntu as my main distribution back during 8.04 and it's been my primary desktop OS since then. Now, I'm going to be looking for a new distro.

Let me break down my opinion:

1) Usability. People like Ubuntu because the typical OS becomes bloated as MS and Apple strive to come up with new features to sell a new version to make more money that just end up unused and getting in the way. People come to Ubuntu to get away from that. Like it or not, the perception is that Ubuntu is better because it doesn't have those kind of initiatives. 11.10 is a complete failure here. The desktop doesn't have simple work flows or an intuitive interface anymore. It's all look and no feel.

2) Flash/Composite over-kill. The same thing happened to KDE when it went to 4, which is now a trainwreck. What is the point? The desktop worked really well before all of the beveled edges and effect-laden Compiz "enhancements". Special effects should COMPLIMENT the experience, NOT *BE* the experience. This is not hard to understand. 10.04 without Unity was as perfect as you should have gotten. Why does Ubuntu, KDE and Gnome keep trying to be OSX?

3) Unity is user interface nightmare. This thing was a horrible idea to begin with and it never worked well even on netbooks. Integrating the menu into the top panel? Seriously? The OSX bar thing on the left pops out unnecessarily and just generally gets in the way more than it helps. Nothing integrates properly; chat, email, calendar...none of it.

4) Customization. I've used gconf-editor more than I've used the "System Settings" in 11.10. No, really. One of the reasons that people always hear and want to use Linux for is the ability to completely customize their experience. How many options did they remove in this version? 100+? Give me a break.

5) Light weight. This is the first time that I've seen Windows run faster and more less choppy than a Linux distro. I never thought that I'd type those words. The fan on my laptop hasn't shut off since I booted. That's just pathetic.

All in all, I have to say: Why can't they leave a good thing alone? Seriously. Instead of fixing the minor issues that were still lingering, they changed the whole thing. Now I need to re-learn how to use my computer again for the 2000th time. Thanks.

---

### Post by cosx on 2011-10-19
I feel betrayed and confused; quite guilty, and most definitely embarrassed, considering the fact that I've personally recommended this distro to quite a number of close and not so close individuals.

I dread to think of what they first thought when they were naive enough to listen to the machine and do the upgrade - and most being the normal individual on the street, the type that Ubuntu apparently is seeking to serve, will have to, by understandable ignorance, accept these changes by force. 

I feel like an idiot myself for being foolish enough to give the devs a change.

If they were going to make it shiny and easy to use, they should have at least done it properly - what kind of usability tests, if any, did they perform to think that any normal individual could find any of this easy and simple to use?

I won't even bother ranting anymore. I'm honestly just disgusted.
 
I know this is not the exact place for this, indeed, it's unlikely that the guys behind it all will even see this post, but I appreciate all that you've done over these years, but frankly, I can't put up with these new changes anymore - and don't think I'll ever be willing to trust this community again.

I feel a case of the decline, that which happens to all communities, is occurring, and thus, only expect things to get worse. Overreacting, sure, but that's all it takes sometimes.

### Post by ARooster on 2011-10-19
After having upgraded from 11.04 to 11.10 I noticed the thing just feels  sort of sluggish. It starts up slow and applications take awhile to run  and the graphical effects just feel sort of slow. As I'm not the  biggest fan of Microsoft's Ribbon or Apple's Dock (as I can read I don't  need to rely exclusively on pictogrammes to ease my use of a computer),  I tried the GNOME 3 classic desktop, but found it quite unstable and no  faster.

I then decided to give Unity a go, as it does seem to be  the way of the future, and decided to do a fresh install of 11.10.  There was no improvement in the sluggishness department but at least the  apps don't crash anymore. I am annoyed that it takes quite longer to  start up or shut down than 10.10 or 11.04. I am also quite surprised to  see that just running Mozilla and System Monitor I am using up well  above 500MB of RAM, especially given the fact the upcoming release of  Windows is allegedly going to be quite streamlined and allegedly using  up in the area of 300MB of RAM (though I'll still have to see that to  believe it). It also uses up quite a bit of processing power, compared  even to Windows (except for Vista).

I am still giving Unity a  try, however I must say I'm not impressed with the Dash. It seems more  of a "for dummies" thing with pretty pictures and nice scrolling,  however it pops up too often. When I'm using my mouse on the left side  of the screen I can sometimes inadvertently hit the screen's edge and  run an application by accident. It also doesn't seem a great idea that  it contains everything, including the rubbish bin/trash and workspace  selector. I filled the Dash with not even nearly all the applications I  normally use so I'm already scrolling, I imagine in a day or two it'll  be even fuller. It'd be nice to have Nautilus, the rubbish bin and  console on a separate part of the screen. There is loads of real estate  now left empty on my screen since I am not using Ubuntu on a mobile  phone, I am sure I could spare a few pixels on my screen for it.

I  will attempt to get used to Unity, apparently it is the future (in  spite of me not being a fan of progress for progress' sake), I have to  say I cannot recommend it to anyone, I do hope this is just a glitch in  the otherwise rational and well thought out evolution of Ubuntu.

### Post by kohoutek1 on 2011-10-19
I've switched now almost totally to KDE in 11.10. Buggy as that is, there's some great work going on there, and in 4.7.2,  KDE has improved by quantum leaps over the last couple of years in ease and options for configuring.

It's found a niche, too, in the desktop UI with its "workspace" tool, including the "activities" dashboard. I like the little workspace tab that stays in the upper right corner of the desktop. Haven't seen that anywhere else. If there's an analogue in Windows, I don't know about it, since I never use Windows. There's certainly not one in Mac, which is where I'm coming from.

Btw, no sweat making KDE look and feel like Mac OS X. The global menubar was easy to find and install, just like it was in Gnome 2.x. Awn and Cairo both work great as docks.

One caveat: Things break easily and often in KDE, even now, so be prepared for more bumps than in Unity or even than in Gnome. Ctrl-S is your best friend.

But I enjoy the excitement and I'll take the flexibility any day.

### Post by viperdvman on 2011-10-19
Do remember one thing: This is a **much bigger** change from 11.04 to 11.10 than the change from 10.10 to 11.04. Sure, when 11.04 came out, it introduced Unity as the primary desktop environment, and it had its teething problems (I've never had any problems with it). But 11.04's Unity was still built on top of GNOME 2, using GTK+ 2, built on a 2.6.38 kernel vs. Mavrick's 2.6.35. Other than Unity, some app changes, and a few other appearance changes, there wasn't much difference between 10.10 and 11.04.

However, 11.10's Unity (its sole DE now), is built on top of GNOME 3, using the GTK+ 3 engine, and the Linux 3.0.4 kernel. **Big difference**. The older stuff was pretty tried-and-true. Sure it had its bugs, but not nearly as many as the newer stuff seems to be having with a number of people, especially those who're upgrading. I'm not pointing that out to defend the devs and Canonical, I'm just explaining why a lot seem to be having problems when upgrading from 11.04 to 11.10.

On a different note, I got a link here to a Desktop Linux Reviews review of Ubuntu 11.10:
[http://desktoplinuxreviews.com/2011/10/18/ubuntu-11-10/](http://desktoplinuxreviews.com/2011/10/18/ubuntu-11-10/)

This guy seemed to have very few problems, if any, with Oneric. And a number of people in this thread alone have had very few if any problems with their installs (most of them clean installs). So yeah... 11.10 is a **very big** change from 11.04, even bigger than the change from 10.10 to 11.04. So there may be some teething issues for a number of us. Hope we all can get it to work out... and hope 12.04 LTS cleans up a lot of the mess a lot of people are having.

As for me, I'm gonna give the persistent LiveUSB another shot, and hopefully not have the same problems on my desktop as last time (it ran flawlessly on my netbook). And I still plan on an early November weekend for installing it alongside my 11.04.

### Post by Norwal on 2011-10-20
I've been using Ubuntu since 6.10 and I have never seen this kind of an uproar over an upgrade.  I have tried both 11.04 and 11.10 on my laptop (Samsung R580) and 11.04 on my desktop. It didn't take me long to change to Classic on my desktop. I found Unity difficult to navigate and restrictive when it came to personalize the desktop. For this reason I hesitated in my migration to 11.10 on my desktop.  

I decided to start with 11.10 on my laptop and was please to see that the button at the top of the big bar of big icons that open even bigger rows of big icons was much improved.  The search improvement did make things easier to find but I just couldn't figure out why they replaced an easier to use drop down menu tree of applications for a mess of big buttons?  Overall I still found Unity clunky and awkward to use. I agree with the assessments on the forums that suggest that devs are getting ready for giant desktop androids. I'll be sticking with a hard drive for as long as I can, thank you very much.  

I decided to install KDE as I used it with SuSE before a friend told me about Ubuntu.  While I liked the look, it had glitches right from the start.  The biggest problem for me was I couldn't get multiple desktops to work. The Pager apt was not on the bar and it would not install. This could have been because I didn't do a clean install of Kubutu.  I may just tried that and see if Kde soots me.

After Kde I decide to do a clean install of 11.04 on my laptop and go with Classic. It's a new Samsung R580 that has never had Linux installed.  The problem now?  Realtek 8192e is not supported and not working. The funny thing is it was working, all be it erratically, with 11.10.  This kind of defeats the point of having a laptop for me. If I'm going to be stuck next to the router then I'll just use my desktop. I tried a few of the forum fixes but I might just stick with Windows 7 for now. I'm just not interested in trouble shooting the drivers right now. 

I have been very happy with Ubuntu, it has been a great OS that has been very stable with a great mix of usability and flexibility. But unfortunately for the Ubuntu devs, gnome has decided to change and this means they have to move on as well. I just don't think, for me, Unity is the right direction.  I will continue to use 10.04 or 11.04 Classic until the last possible moment and we'll see how things develop.  After all 11.10 is much improved over 11.04.

[*]Dolphin looks just a little bit sleeker. Not much difference from 11.04's Dolphin.
[*]I can't really comment on Kontact since I don't use it in both Kubuntu LiveUSB and my Ubuntu's KDE desktop.
[*]I also don't use Amarok either, preferring the lighter weight Audacious over it. I could give Amarok a chance since my friend in Australia seems to like it.
[*]I do like the "breadcrumb" feature in the main menu (e.g. Games > Card Games > KPatience) so I can easily go back all the way if I want to.
[*]And the Muon Software Center... it's nice. It isn't quite as good as Ubuntu Software Center, but it's better than what Kubuntu 11.04 has, and has a much nicer interface.
[*]I haven't messed with the Muon Package Manager yet, nor have I messed with the "low-fat settings" yet.
[/LIST]
Overall, I like Kubuntu 11.10 quite a bit. I also like running the KDE Plasma desktop/netbook environment (yes, I like both of them). I even have a full KDE desktop and netbook environment installed alongside my Unity and GNOME 2.x on my Ubuntu 11.04 install *(my primary Linux)*.

So yes, Kubuntu 11.10 in my opinion is definitely a homerun. However, everyone is getting me curious about Xubuntu... so I might try it and Xfce out as well.

---

### Post by bmathise on 2011-10-20
> **crdlb said:**
> Before you do that, you might want to give this a try: [https://help.ubuntu.com/community/GnomeClassic](https://help.ubuntu.com/community/GnomeClassic) . It's not GNOME 2, but it may be close enough to justify not reinstalling 11.04.
I'm back on 11.04, and what a relief! I just 
- noted which applications I had installed
- backed up the home directory with hidden files and all
- reinstalled 11.04 from scratch
- installed all the applications I had before
- installed all updates (NOT the dist upgrade, though!! :) )
- copied the home directory back and made sure to overwrite all conflicting files
- restarted and logged in using the Ubuntu standard no effects session (you may correct me if I don't remember the session name correctly)

And, hey presto, I have my beloved Gnome 2 back. Good as new. The whole procedure took me about 1.5 hour and now I'm happy :)

However, this new interface is less intuitive and efficient while also limiting choices in customization and multi-tasking ease.  People don't like feeling that developers don't care about their concerns - only their vision of what they want users to adapt to.

So, personally, I am criticizing more than hating, and hoping the critiques help Ubuntu get back on the right track.

Windows, the No. 1 operating system in the world is successful precisely because it has a familiar interface that extends across all Microsoft products. People who come to Windows know the look and feel and how it operates. I think its reasonable for Canonical to have a similar vision for Ubuntu. A single interface that every one who comes to know how it looks and how it will operate. The market Ubuntu is looking for aren't techies. They are average folks who just want something that works. This is how Ubuntu is getting back on track to break into the computing mainstream.

For better then, Unity is here to stay.

### Post by EricZartan on 2011-10-21
Unity sucks!
I'm very sorry, I am a fan of positive critique and do not belive in negative criticism at all. But it had to be said.
After upgrading my already beautifully working 11.04 to 11.10, my heart stopped. Slow boot times, slow start of everything, once again- an upgrade "improved" the system by slowing it down and making everything harder to find and less intuitive.

However I am a hardcore Linux fan (Ubuntu is still my #1) so I installed:
Gnome shell
LXDE
XFCE

Gnome-
The new gnome is great- like it much better than unity, although not necessarily better than the old gnome. I really should have stuck to the maxim "IF IT'S NOT BROKEN - DON'T FIX IT!".
But the new Gnome is not necessarily faster than Unity, just more intuitive and easier to use , so because of the logistics it's faster.

LXDE-
The speed winner hands down! Has anyone else noticed how much faster LXDE is than the rest?! Try it! I'm not just talking app's. but also websites etc. Try a website that is typically slow, in LXDE there is a measurable difference- it is so much faster than gnome or unity that I am using it as my normal/default desktop now.
LXDE is definately the unsung heroe for me.

XFCE-
also very nice- very logical and usable with intuitive overview and very customizable. I highle recommend XFCE also, BUT still not as fast as LXDE.

HOWEVER - all of these Desktop UI's are faster than Unity!

My machine is no slouch- an Acer Travelmate w/dual core, 2ghz 4mb ram, just bought it this year too.

I'm sorry Unity, you do not win even in one category compared to the rest.

And next time there is an upgrade I will definately think twice thrice and 4 times before doing it. 

If your system is working fine - why upgrade?
Seriously think about this before your next upgrade.

Z.

### Post by ARooster on 2011-10-21
> **koleoptero said:**
> That points to the fact that the default interface should be easy and familiar. The lack of customization options doesn't help, noone will force anyone to customize the desktop, but if someone wants to then it should be there.

Anyway both unity and gnome 3 are relatively new, things will smoothen out in the future.

And not every windows user is happy with the interface, there are many popular hacks and apps that allow you to customize further, and lots of people use them.


Couldn't agree with you more. It is unfamiliar to both a new and an existing Ubuntu user. I do believe the similarity between gnome's desktop and the Windows desktops (which were essentially the same from 95 on) was the main reason Ubuntu became that successful... well that and the fact Vista was a complete miss.

I get annoyed with some Ubuntu zealots who keep saying Unity is great and even easier and faster to use than the old and familiar gnome 2.x. I believe the reasoning is we're incompetent because it works great if you use keyboard shortcuts and you can start typing into the Dash and it will bring up all the applications, unlike the old and cluttered menus of gnome 2 that were organised in a (or at least I thought so) rather smartly thought out manner. Unity is only familiar to people who use smartphones a lot. Well, personally I do use a smartphone a lot... unfortunately mostly for calling or texting people, I take the odd picture and sometimes I surf the web with it. No matter what, I generally use it to do one thing at a time, while on a desktop computer I will typically run in the area of 10 or 20 applications at once with several instances of some of those running applications. This is where the bottom bar of the old gnome came in handy, to keep track of all these windows and to switch between them with ease. The dock bar ducks out of the way when you've got a window maximised, however, so you have to bring it up by moving mouse to the left and it doesn't tell you a thing. It's unfamiliar, impractical and perhaps useful for a touch screen device, however as things stand now, I believe a tablet computer's user would have the keyboard open 50% of the time (because, as the fanboys keep telling us, we need to use keyboard shortcuts and type into the Dash home).

### Post by Dragonbite on 2011-10-21
Some things work better, others not so good.

[LIST]
[*]I find Unity (2D) slower than Gnome 2 or Unity 2D on 10.04
[*]Bootup time takes longer. While it is quick to getting to enter your password, it takes longer than it used to to bring up the Dash and be functional (I haven't compared it, but it seems even longer than KDE on the same machine
[*]It looks pretty good, and that is with Unity 2D because my system can't handle 3D.
[*]I don't like the additional steps to opening a program without having to type it.  The old menu bar made most programs open in 2-3.
[*]I wish it was more configurable, but I understand this is the beginning and in time it should get more open.
[*]I like the added real-estate and how maximized, the application takes more of the screen then before. On a 12" laptop, this makes a difference.
[*]I like the wallpapers included (as always)
[*]Unity is a bit slow, but I have changed its focus
[*]Deja Dup (and Ubuntu One) makes it easy to backup the system
[*]The software store (center?) is slow to boot up. It looks good, but is slow
[*]My webcam doesn't work, even with the env_preload line.  This is the first distro to fully not support it.  It works in Kubuntu though (go figure :confused:)
[/LIST]

Well, that's what I can think of off the top of my head.  I wanted to avoid getting into a Unity bitch-fest.

KBD47, actually, I think just PITA is an even better description ;)

BigSilly, I don't run 10 to 20 apps on Ubuntu anymore either. I just switch to Ubuntu to do what I need it for (though I have to admit that in the passed week I've been trying to give Unity a chance and did not yet bother to tweak GRUB to start in the alternative OS as yet... though this is mainly because I'm considering going back one or two Ubuntu releases) and run the few applications I use it for, mostly just a couple at a time. The one I'm actively using plus Mozilla, Thunderbird and Skype in the background, though even this is a pain as when you full-screen an application the added screen space in fact makes it more of a hassle to find the Skype window if someone decides to tell you something while you're working on something else in full screen mode.

While I've been mostly using a different OS as an alternative because of a few programmes that will not run on Linux up until this release the radical change has in fact reversed this. As Ubuntu is a popular choice among PC users I am sticking with it for awhile, however I've got my doubts about it continuing the growth in its user base as this radical change is not something everyone is embracing easily. Not even the big OS makers are arrogant enough to just assume their users will embrace radical change simply because their new release is pretty and shiny. And well, given the poor performance energy saving-wise and lack of essential functionality for a tablet I don't really think the expansion into the tablet market will happen so my prediction would be a decline in Ubuntu's overall user base. A shame really since upto now it was a solid and steadily growing desktop computer player.

---

### Post by bwalton on 2011-10-21
I recently updated to 11.10 -- what a disaster!  The user interface is practically unusable.  It is like an interface for kindergartners, or maybe designed by kindergartners.  Menus for windows aren't in the window.  I have no idea how to fire up most of my applications -- they don't appear in the selection menu because it is limited to just a few. In trying to back it out (I don't know what I'm doing in that regime), it got so all I got upon booting was a gray screen.  I reloaded the version of Ubuntu I started with in October 2010.  I am still installing software, getting settings right, etc.  And now the microphone doesn't work (it always did before, and still does when booting to other operating systems).  I learned my lesson -- no more upgrades without thorough research first.

### Post by NormanFLinux on 2011-10-21
> **VTPoet said:**
> You can also install a classic menu indicator in either 2D (I think) or 3D:

[How to install ClassicMenu Indicator on ubuntu 11.10 (Oneiric Ocelot) | Ubuntu Geek]("http://www.ubuntugeek.com/how-to-install-classicmenu-indicator-on-ubuntu-11-10-oneiric-ocelot.html")

There is no need to log in and log out of the Unity session to see the menu. It works much the same as the Classic Menu port to Windows 7. I prefer to look than to type to find what I want. It just works for me.
