### Post by NormanFLinux on 2011-10-21
> **keithpeter said:**
> Hello All

Is there a beginner's guide to Unity written for people that might boot Ubuntu? Is there one that has been updated for 11.10? 

Does anyone else remember the old animated introduction to Windows 95?

Or something like the Kindle Guide?

Its intuitive. Most people are familiar with various kinds of launchers and the difference is Unity is on the left rather than on the bottom of the screen. It took me a short learning curve to figure out the dock in Mac OSX. Once I did, I liked it.

---

### Post by NormanFLinux on 2011-10-21
> **ARooster said:**
> Oh, and seriously, why does an application that's not full screen-ed need a global menu?

The idea is that all apps share the same menu. Its simply a consolidation of the application menu and they're the same in all of them. And if you need workspaces, there's that in Ubuntu - works much like its counterpart in Mac OSX. There is a third party workspace software available for Windows 7.

---

### Post by Pithikos on 2011-10-21
> **NormanFLinux said:**
> The idea is that all apps share the same menu. Its simply a consolidation of the application menu and they're the same in all of them. And if you need workspaces, there's that in Ubuntu - works much like its counterpart in Mac OSX. There is a third party workspace software available for Windows 7.

I find that a really bad design. For a home user like me working on a big screen and having my programs floating around it is a DISASTER. It's times when I need to see more than one app at the same time. Then workspaces are not usable anymore.

---

### Post by viperdvman on 2011-10-21
I see quite a number of people ranting about the Global App Menu in Unity *(it was there in 11.04's Unity too, available to add to 11.04's GNOME 2.x out of the box, and available for download from 10.04 onward to my knowledge)*. I personally like the global app menu. It's great for freeing up some screen real estate on smaller monitors like my netbook. Plus, I like the Mac-style interface. _The only ones who would really complain about it, IMHO, are former or hardcore Windows users who're used to having their menus **in** the Window._ I don't see any Mac users complaining.

Back to the topic:

[SIZE=3]Warning: Long and thought-out post ahead[/SIZE]... might make for a review too [COLOR=Red](formatting editted in response to a few posters to make it easier on the eyes)[/COLOR]

I did a rather extended test drive of Ubuntu 11.10 last night on a fresh persistent LiveUSB session, doing most of the various stuff that I normally do. I installed the **ubuntu-restricted-extras**, **audacious**, **vlc**, **pidgin**, and a few other stuff that I run a lot on Ubuntu that didn't come on the LiveDVD image. I also took the time to install **gnome-shell**, **gnome-tweak**, and **CCSM** for compiz. I know I installed a few other things as well, but I'm not on it right now so I'm not looking. So after a night of test driving on my _desktop computer_, here are my observations:


_Unity:_  I really like how Unity 3D looks, and how it acts. I like all the transparency effects in both the Launcher and the Dash... and how the borders of the Dash connected very well into the launcher and top panel to create a very fluid look. It definitely looks much better than 11.04's Unity. However, the full-screen Dash's close, max, min buttons weren't there for some strange reason like they were when I test drove the Beta 2 *(then again, it didn't run on my netbook first like the Beta 2 did)*. They were there when I test drove the release on my netbook, however. Also, I'm running the open-source nouveau for NVidia graphics which generally sucks. But that's only a minor aesthetic gripe. I like how easy it is for me to find my App List categories (the second lens to the left on the bottom of the dash)... how they take up the right side of the screen. Those filtering options really do help if you don't want to go through the whole App list. It's really no different that clicking on GNOME Shell's "Applications" next to the Windows after clicking on Activities. The only extra click over GNOME Shell is clicking on "Show all xx number of applications". All my previous minor gripes with Unity (some totally shot down as I was able to do the same thing in 11.04's Unity) were totally solved in this release. The new Alt-Tab worked great, but didn't look as pretty on the desktop (blame nouveau drivers again) as it did on my netbook. But on my netbook, I really liked it and liked how I can press the down key to preview it (which can be tweaked, if I remember right). I had no problem finding minimized windows in the Launcher (same as in 11.04), as it's really no different than finding minimized windows in my 11.04's AWN dock. I also had no problem adding and removing Launchers either. However, it did require me opening the app in order to add it as a launcher. A "right-click>add to launcher" would be nice. I was also able to arrange the icons easily by clicking and holding for 0.5-1s so I could move the icon. The Launcher is just another fancy dock that's totally integrated into Unity. Bottom line, Unity was starting to grow on be back in 11.04, and it looks and acts even better in 11.10. I like running it, and won't mind losing my GNOME 2.x when Natty and Lucid support ends and I probably adopt either 12.04 LTS or whatever is newest at the time.  I didn't run Unity 2D during last night's test drive, so no comments on that.

[COLOR=DarkRed]_Unity gripes (minor):_[/COLOR]  I'm running the open-source **nouveau** graphics driver. Unlike on my netbook *(which runs an ATI Radeon 4250 HD)*, Unity on my desktop seems to run a little bit slower. Not drastically, but noticeably. Mainly, the Compiz effects don't work, and the moving of windows is a tad bit slow. Then again, Compiz doesn't run well on the nouveau driver anyway (it runs on my netbook's open-source radeon driver). Only a minor issue. Hope to have that fixed with a full install and proprietary NVidia driver.

[COLOR=DarkRed]_Additional Drivers gripe (minor):_[/COLOR]  Speaking of proprietary drivers, the LiveUSB session's Additional Drivers can't seem to locate said NVidia drivers, neither the current nor legacy. I didn't have that problem with the Beta 2 on LiveUSB session... then again, the legacy drivers didn't install on that either. Then again, I've never installed proprietary drivers on a LiveUSB session before then, so I don't know if that's a norm. I just hope I'll be able to install the *nvidia-current *drivers on a full-install.

**[COLOR=Red]_User Accounts (major gripe warning):_[/COLOR]** I am almost completely unable to create and edit users in the LiveUSB session. On most persistent LiveUSB's, especially Ubuntu, I like to create a second user so I can access the other Desktop environments since I don't know the password for the LiveUSB user. I was able to create a user from the User Accounts settings, but once it created, it crashed. I go into User Accounts again, and as soon as I click on the created user to edit the settings, it crashed again. I had to set the password for the account by command line in the Terminal. I hope this isn't an issue in the full install. If so, **PLEASE FIX the User Accounts settings**.

_Ubuntu Software Center:_ I like how the new Ubuntu Software Center looks. It definitely continues to be one of, if not THE, best basic package managers/app centers out there. I've had no problems finding and adding all the packages I usually add when test driving or after installing a Linux distro. It looks so much nicer and so much sleeker than 11.04's Ubuntu Software Center. I don't have any gripes about it at all.

_Eye of GNOME:_ It too looks prettier than 11.04's EoG, yet doesn't loose any of the functionality that the old one had. Overall... kudos, I like it.

_LightDM:_ Granted, I have no idea how to customize it like I can with GDM on Ubuntu 11.04 and earlier, but I still like it. It's much sleeker and easier to use than GDM is, in my opinion. I like how easy it is to change desktop environments, since the button for it is right next to the user login instead of on a bottom panel. Speaking of other desktop environments...

**[COLOR=Red]_LightDM gripes (major gripe warning):_[/COLOR]**[COLOR=DarkRed][COLOR=Black] I don't know if this is LiveUSB-related or not, but when I go to shutdown Ubuntu, it takes me straight to the Login screen. That's only a min[/COLOR][/COLOR]or gripe, as Fedora 15 does the exact same thing... in fact, by default, Fedora 15 only has suspend and logout, you'd have to shut down from the Login screen. However, when I go to shutdown or restart in the Login screen, it **does absolutely nothing**. It doesn't shut down, it doesn't end the X session, it doesn't even error. I had to go into the Terminal and type *sudo shutdown -r now* to restart my computer. Like I said, I hope this is LiveUSB related. If this is true on the full install... **please fix the shutdown features in at least the login screen.**

_GNOME Shell:_ I also installed GNOME Shell just to play around with a newer version of it, and in an Ubuntu environment, than Fedora 15's. I actually like GNOME 3.2 better than GNOME 3.0. I had much less problems running it on Ubuntu 11.10 than I did running it on Fedora 15. It ran both smoother (graphics performance) and more stable (much fewer bugs and GNOME crash reports) than Fedora's GNOME Shell did. I was easily able to search through the apps, clear the favorites and arrange new ones how I wanted, and run multiple Windows on it. I also like how I was able to use **gnome-tweak** to add (Sun Oct 20) to the time on the top panel and add the minimize and maximize buttons to the Window Title bars. I like it so far, more so than Fedora's. I will definitely install it when I do a full install of 11.10 alongside my 11.04 (which will remain my primary Linux as I won't let go of my GNOME 2's Mac OS X style interface very easily). I'm just glad that 11.04 **didn't** adopt GNOME 3.0 like Fedora did right out the gate. I didn't run GNOME Classic during last night's test drive, so I can't comment on it.

_Compiz Settings (netbook only):_ Since Compiz doesn't work well on the desktop with its nouveau driver, I'll go ahead and comment about the Compiz and other customization settings that I experimented with on the netbook last weekend. Note, my netbook was running the open-source **radeon** driver, which works great with Compiz, even on my 11.04 install. I like the boat load of extra settings there are for Unity. I can actually mess around with the Launcher opacity and probably a few other Launcher and Dash related stuff too. I was even able to set transparency for the top panel quite easily from Compiz... which can't be done in GNOME 2.x without switching to background color (which makes the rest of Ambiance and Radiance suck on those panels, so I never use it in 11.04). Of course, I like to add my Window effects for Opening, Closing, Menus, Dropdowns, Popups, Tooltips, Minimizing/Restoring, etc... and they worked like a charm. Note, I didn't explore all of the Compiz settings... just the ones pertaining to Unity and what I normally use in my 11.04 install.


I think that would be all for now. That's much of what I found on just one long night of an extended test drive of Ubuntu 11.10 on a LiveUSB session. As I posted previously in this thread, I had persistence issues with the LiveUSB and volume-mounting issues from my first test run on the desktop (the later was probably user permissions related issues... or somehow the persistence corrupted due to shutdown issues). In last night's run, other than the gripes I posted above, I had no issues running 11.10 on my desktop in a LiveUSB session. Everything worked like it was supposed to, my sound worked great, my videos played very nicely, my music played well, YouTube played well, and I even downloaded Lubuntu 11.10 and a few open-source apps in Transmission. I really had no issues except those few minor and a couple major gripes (one crash with User Accounts and Shutdown doing absolutely nothing).

I like 11.10 a lot, but I will still stick with 11.04 as my primary since I like what I have on it already. I'll install 11.10 alongside 11.04 and keep it there for a good while, mainly to play with running my usual stuff in both the newer Unity and in GNOME Shell and GNOME Classic. Also, you get the best experience with a full-install over a LiveUSB session. I'll post more as I play with the LiveUSB session some more.

---

So all those identified as using Ubuntu 11.04 are actually using Chromium or one of the other browsers. The last data point is impossible to gauge now and it’s probably not that low. Still an estimated 15% of users aren’t using Firefox, not bad.

### Post by jfreak_ on 2011-10-22
I was using the LTS version since the time it was released, and I thought that unity has matured so why not try it out...
Here are my thoughts

Installation: 

1. Apparently the boot up process still doesn't play nice with the nvidia cards. I had to enable the nomodeset option to get the installation started. Again after installation on the first restart, I had to navigate to the grub menu, edit it to include the nomodeset option (i Had forgotten that the shift key brings up the grub menu on start up, so had to go to a friends room to look it up :(  )

First Use:

2. Very slow (see signature for system specs), especially when my external hard drive was plugged in. Htop reported that Lightdm was eating up cpu cycles. 

3. I loved the music indicator applet, made my life more easier. 

4. Ubuntu Software Centre is much more responsive that the one in 10.04, although I always use Synaptic Package Manager and had to download it first. 

5. The Dash is useless if I don't know the name of the program. I spent a lot of time hunting where to set proxy setting for Ubuntu Software Centre.

6. If I am working in a particular window and I want to close another window I have to focus that window and then close it. Two clicks instead of one.

7. Haven't found out where the Show Desktop thingy is.

8. Some the panel on the left side refuses to appear when I move my mouse towards it and I am forced to minimize all the windows one by one to get it. (Hence the need for Show Desktop)

9. No support for Wine applications. That is they are not searchable in the Dash and I haven't figured how to put the shortcut on the left-side panel . A very important aspect since I am dependant on MS Office and refuse to use OpenOffice .

Remedial Measures:

10. Installed Lubuntu. Fast, responsive and does all my stuff without getting in my way , and supports wine menus. 

Last thoughts: 

11 . Was literally shouting and screaming at my laptop screen until I moved to Lubuntu.  :D

### Post by rogprov on 2011-10-22

### Post by markbl on 2011-10-22
I think unity may have potential but there are so many bugs that usability is compromised too much to form an opinion. I skipped 11.04 as I thought it too immature but have jumped now from 10.10 to 11.10.

Popups sometimes don't grab focus, chrome gets double window title bars after return from maximised, the launcher sometimes "loses" running apps, gwibber is so brain-dead I can't believe ubuntu promote it, unity "aero-banding" randomly moves the mouse cursor as you hold the title bar, sometimes you can't select any window, apps shutdown when greater than 75% of screen get maximised when started again (that apparently is a design choice!), ubuntu has removed the "custom" preferred email client which many of us used to set web gmail, but have not offered an alternative, dual screen title bars often can not be viewed unless you hold the mouse button down, etc, etc, etc. All infuriating bugs I have noticed in about 1 day of use.

### Post by Pithikos on 2011-10-22
> **viperdvman said:**
> I see quite a number of people ranting about the Global App Menu in Unity *(it was there in 11.04's Unity too, available to add to 11.04's GNOME 2.x out of the box, and available for download from 10.04 onward to my knowledge)*. I personally like the global app menu. It's great for freeing up some screen real estate on smaller monitors like my netbook. Plus, I like the Mac-style interface. _The only ones who would really complain about it, IMHO, are former or hardcore Windows users who're used to having their menus **in** the Window._ I don't see any Mac users complaining.

The buttons on the left side of the window bars(like in Mac) are good indeed as 80% of the time the mouse is on the left side(especially when the Unity bar is there). The mouse goes towards upper-left practically only when you want to shutdown the computer or logout.

Ubuntu has gone off the deep end with Unity imho. They are dictating placement of things on a foss desktop. You are going to get people leaving. ---

### Post by Kilz on 2011-10-23
> **uRock said:**
> Why would people need to take away from the system? Unity was meant to refine gnome3. Gnome3 is easily set up and the fall back package is only one command away, so I do not see where the problem is. gnome 2 is history and there is nothing Canonical could do about that, but make the best of it.

There are other DEs for those who prefer the old gnome look.

Some people are like sheep. They use the default install, perhaps changing the wallpaper or the colors. Others like myself never leave anything alone. For an example you might want to look at the screenshot in [this  post.]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=11379489&postcount=445")
I have been using Ubuntu since Dapper Drake. At one time it was my favorite distro. No more, I moved to Mint because they had a Gnome 2 desktop while I figured out what my next step is. Since they announced moving the Gnome-shell I figured it was time to see if I could make something work.
To tell the truth I knew people would leave when I saw Unity for the first time. Unity is as much a disaster as is Gnome 3. Neither is usable for work, the flow of the desktop is horrible. The desktop is frozen and that in itself is a disaster in an open source operating system. One of the great things about open source is the ability to change things. Otherwise you might as well just run Windows.
Conical could have done something else, like fork and improve the existing Gnome 2 code. But that wasnt going to happen. Instead what they did was follow the lead of Gnome Shell and build a horror that is best suited to phones and netbooks. Only thing is, a lot of people still have desktops and full sized laptops like myself.

I now use 11.10, but its not Unity, and its not Gnome Shell. To tell the truth I love being able to install exactly what I want, and set everything exactly as I want from the start. Instead of deleting and changing whats there. I started with a command line system and added what I wanted. But I have a feeling thats to much to ask for the average Ubuntu user.

Keep telling yourself that Unity is the best thing since sliced bread, perhaps you will even believe it one day, or convince someone else it is. But I foresee a lot of users trying other distros to find something workable. That in itself isnt a bad thing.

---

### Post by viperdvman on 2011-10-24
I just did something a little odd, but I actually did a full install of Ubuntu 11.10 on one of my larger USB Flash Drives. And right now, it's working, and working pretty decently. Granted, it's not going to run as fast being on a USB Flash drive rather than a SATA HDD or SATA SSD, but it works.

It seems like my onboard NVidia 7050 doesn't work with this version of Ubuntu very well since it likes to fallback to Unity 2D, even with the legacy and current NVidia drivers. I only say the onboard NVidia 'cause it's what Linux likes to favor over the Nvidia 6800 GT. So yeah, I won't be using Unity 3D on the desktop until I replace the 6800 GT with at least a strong dual-head 200-series and disable the onboard. I could disable the onboard and just use the 6800 GT (which is stronger), but I'd lose my dual displays in Windows (which I do use for gaming). And because I ended up being in Unity 2D, Compiz settings don't work for it either. I haven't fully explored GNOME Shell yet, but I will say that GNOME Shell is running much more smoothly with the proprietary driver on this full install than on the LiveUSB with the nouveau driver. :)

The gripes I had about LightDM not shutting down seemed to have been solved. I don't know if the full install did it or the fact that I downloaded a bunch of updates right after install. But both the Unity desktop and the LightDM login, I can shutdown or restart very easily. I haven't tried creating users yet, but then again why do I need to?

**[COLOR=DarkRed]Gripe alert:[/COLOR]** I have run into problems with LibreOffice in Unity 2D. If I maximize the window, good luck restoring it. Because for some reason, the window controls do not appear in the left-hand side of the top panel like they should (like all my other maximized apps do). That, and the lo-menubar doesn't work with it either (had to sudo apt-get remove it). I had to move into GNOME Shell to remove it. Any solutions to the LibreOffice problem in Unity 2D? (I'll search for a thread on this subject).

So far so good. I'll keep a LiveUSB around for use on my netbook since it seems to run Oneric better than my desktop does (I blame my desktop's oddball NVidia setup for that).

### Post by viperdvman on 2011-10-24
I say it more and more often, and it's even in my signature.

If you don't like Unity, or even GNOME 3 Shell, there are other DE's even within the official Ubuntu family (KDE, Xfce, LXDE). Ubuntu gives us choices, something not every distro does. Find one that suits you and move on.

GNOME 2 is dead, though I've heard it's being or has been forked (hasn't really caught on yet). So it's only gonna be a matter of a few more years before the last of even the smaller distros switch over to GNOME 3 and most likely GNOME Shell.

### Post by MooPi on 2011-10-24
I read through 29 pages of this thread before I decided to jump to the end and post a comment. I read to 29 because I wanted to get a feel for what everyone was feeling and thinking of the new release. Didn't think reading further would enlighten me more so it's time to post. There is a definite undertow of discontent of which I'm a part of, likewise there are the steadfast supporters holding firm that Ubuntu has the right ideas and is moving in the right direction. I think Ubuntu is at a crossroads of either failure or success and is teetering amongst the user pool. I have moved on to Debian and am pleased thus far with that experience. I also see that the changes though troublesome to me are easily picked up by new users and those that use cutting edge hardware (new stuff). The new interface uses more resources and works better given the new gear. I can see that Ubuntu wants to be progressive and push the community towards what it believes is the future UI. The trouble with that vision in my estimation is that Ubuntu has moved to lock down the OS more than ever to control the UI. This may be good for the average user but detrimental to skilled and diehard Linux users that enjoy the ability to manipulate the OS and desktop in unimaginable ways. Linux users tend to be mavericks and not the usual cookie cutter walk the line types.  So this new version is objectionable to what I see as the larger Linux community and not the new user pool that Ubuntu is trying to influence. I as a Ubuntu user and past lobbyist , I for one would like to see a release that gives us that control back and still have the core OS and repositories available. This would give old curmudgeons like me the ability to fiddle to our hearts content and help along those new users looking for something other than that cookie cutter OS we are all to familiar with. I joined the Ubuntu community because it gave me ease and function that at the time was unheard of in other distros. This is not the case now and I feel the Ubuntu is going to loose those that helped form that huge following.

---

### Post by Jim_in_Omaha on 2011-10-24
I am working on it. I moved from 11.04 in classic with a down graded Compiz and it was working very nicely.

The one thing I'm kind of liking is the typing in the name of something I'm looking for and it finding the application. Both Unity and Gnome Shell are doing that.

I personally like Gnome Shell over Unity. It seems faster, but there are some nagging problems I will be glad when a fix comes out.

The memory and CPU usage slowly growing after a while. Seems to mainly be happening on my desktop and not the laptop. Left it on last night, this morning 2.9gig and 90%+ on one core. I know I'm not the only one.

Seems like Unity does not have that issue on either system. Unity 2d is fairly well behaved in both issues.

LightDM is nice.

There is so much chatter about it I'm finding difficulty in searching for solutions. 

Q9450
Gigabyte EP35 MB
6gig ram
Nivida GTX470 - I have tried the open source, and am currently on the Beta 285.+
Ocz Vertex2 SSD

Laptop
Thinkpad T500
P8700
Ocz Vertex2 SSD
8gigs
The power management seems to be working fairly good with it. 4+ hours on the big 9cell unit. I bought this thing used so I don't know just how long a new extended battery will run on a T500. Using the integrated Intel graphics, not the switchable discrete AMD to save on power. Although, I get 42fps on Urban Terror with the Intel, I get 90+ with the AMD. But this thing is for school work.

I'm not too picky except for bugs like the CPU and memory. That stuff really bothers me. Most the other things are tweakable enough. I even have Unity almost sort of working like Gnome Shell.

The thing that keeps me with Ubuntu, and I know there are other options, is I really like the UbuntuOne and the Software Center. I give them a big thumbs up for those things.

At school I have had Gnome Shell running on the mighty T500 and it sure does get some looks. Those who have never ventured into the open source world are very curious as to what I'm using. But the number one comment I hear from the more technical types is they stick with Windows because they love their games. I certainly understand.....

### Post by K-Ghidorah on 2011-10-24
Here's my $0.02:

Like many, I defected from main Ubuntu when I first tried 11.04.  Yes, because of Unity.  It felt very clunky and counter-intuitive, and after discovering the degrees of customization of Gnome and the XFCE GUI, the 'static-ness' of Unity just felt wrong.  I also disliked how Canonical seemed to be ramming Unity down their users' throats.

After I borked my Zorin OS install on my desktop (HP with AMD Quad-core and 6 GB of RAM), I figured 'what the hell' and installed Ubuntu 11.10 onto it.  I was so pleased with it that it wasn't too long before my laptop (Dell Latitude D620 with Intel Core Duo with 3GB RAM) got the same treatment.  Unity has come a very long way in a very short amount of time.  I find it quite usable out of the box, and personally, I've witnessed no slowdown at all.  In fact, even my relatively old laptop runs quite zippily with it.  There are a few kinks to be ironed out, some things that I would've liked to see, and only one real WTF moment.  But so far, no dealbreakers (still early yet, though).

The WTF moment... where's my sound card mixer?!  I used to be able to install ALSA mixer to work with my soundcard (I'm a musician and use my compy to record), but I had to download gamix from the website itself and boot it from terminal.  After being able to do it all from the GUI, I found this level of jumping through hoops to be unacceptable, and if I HADN'T been able to work around that, it would've been a real dealbreaker.  A shame, since most of the things Unity had done WRONG in Natty had been ironed out quite nicely.

Also, why was Synaptic left out with the initial install?!  It was easy enough to install and in the end, I suppose it wasn't that big of a deal, but the fact that it was left out seemed like a huge oversight on Canonical's part.

My other minor gripe is that Unity is still far too static.  I've accepted that the dock is going to stay on the left side, as will the window buttons.  I've let it go.  No biggie.   However, is it such a bad thing to let us change the colors and the fonts?!

That said, I can finally see what Canonical intended Unity to be, and it's quite delightful.  The complaint that the dock takes up too much screen-estate is bunk.  Move the window (or blow it up to full screen) and it goes bye-bye.  And it's another little thing, but I like how, when the window is blown up to full screen, it takes up the entire screen and the menus show up when you hover the pointer over it.  It may be old hat to some folks here, but it's new to me and you know what?  I LIKE IT.  And like I said, maneuvering the dock seems to be more fluid now and a lot less clunky.

In the end, it feels good to be back with straight-up Ubuntu.  When I migrated from Windows, it was Ubuntu 9.10 that busted my Linux cherry.  I've used (and loved) Linux Mint, and used Zorin OS 5 for quite a while (that's actually the one I recommend most to Windows refugees... it's a fine distro that shouldn't be overlooked).  And personally, I'm now looking forward to 12.04.  If Unity improved that much over the span of a few months, I can't wait to see how 12.04 will be.

---

### Post by trivialpackets on 2011-10-25
My experience with 11.10 goes like this:
  1) Fresh install of Ubuntu 11.10
  2) I was instantly a fan'
  3) I had some problems connecting with NX which was a showstopper for me.
  4) I then switched to Kubuntu 11.10
  5) NX worked well
  6) I experienced a few crashes in KDE
  7) I never really got into the interface of KDE
  8) I started missing the cohesiveness of Ubuntu + Unity
  9) I switched back to Ubuntu 11.10
  10) I figured out how to use NX on ubuntu 11.10 to pull Unity 2D and now am reasonably satisfied with it.

Using KDE definitely had some "whoa, that's cool" type moments, that is for sure.  It has come a long ways since the last time I used it.  Still working to tweak out the Unity environment to make things super simple for me, but overall I'm settling in very nicely.

### Post by jackdale on 2011-10-26
I really like Unity.  I think that the Dash is more functional than Gnome3 (although Gnome3's is prettier at the moment).  The difficulty in customising the look and feel is surprising, but I hope that this is only a reflexion of the early nature of the release.  At the moment (out of the box) it is only about as configurable as OS X's Aqua.  I like that it runs with Compiz as this is already a very mature compositing framework with lots of eye-candy (for those who think that is important) as well as lots of functional options (again not configurable out of the box, but easy enough to do with a little "apt-getting").  Still, Gnome3 seems to be catching up a little here too. 

There is a lot of work to be done if they want to get it ready for the 12.04 release and I doubt it will be mature by then.  I think it needs another 2 or even 3 release cycles before it becomes what we would all like it to be.

Currently I'm using it with extensive compiz modifications and cairo-dock (what can I say? I can't get enough of launchers!).  I would like to see the dash being able to be located anywhere and be able to serve the same role (as well as look) as other docks like cairo-dock.

I think LXDE should seriously be the new benchmark.
Are you listening Ubuntu developers/team?

### Post by viperdvman on 2011-11-01
> **jarlath said:**
> viperdvman - are you saying you did a desktop install but used a usb stick as the installation partition? That sounds like a cool idea.

Sure did. I did it so I could test a full install without having to redo the partitions. I used the USB *(/dev/sdc for example)* to mount the / and installed GRUB on the same partition. It's sorta like a mobile hard drive. Now I won't be using it with my netbook since it and my desktop are totally different *(Intel & NVidia vs. AMD & ATI)*.

It allowed me to do a much better test drive of Oneric... and not have to deal with the persistence issues and mounting errors (exit code 21), which made the distro almost completely useless.

Like I said, I thoroughly enjoyed using Oneric and the refined Unity, but it doesn't like my oddball onboard 7-series and PCIe 6800GT NVidia chips, so it never boots into Unity 3D. I might install it alongside Natty once I replace the 6800GT with likely a 200-series NVidia [COLOR=DarkOliveGreen]*(I've not heard good stories about the 400-series performance)*[/COLOR][COLOR=Black] and disable the onboard.[/COLOR] A proper dual-head should work much better for both my dual monitors and working the newest Unity.

### Post by philinux on 2011-11-01
> **vasa1 said:**
> Yes, that's what I meant.

Your screenshot is nice but doesn't  it mean that conky occupies real estate all the time?

And have you reduced the size of icons in the Launcher on the left side of the screen? I used CCSM to set the Launcher to appear only when I mouse over there.

No I can drag an open window over conky. These settings below do that. However it prevents me from placing an icon there which is my choice.

```
own_window yes
own_window_type normal
own_window_transparent yes
own_window_hints undecorated,below,skip_taskbar
on_bottom yes
```

And I've reduced the icon size to the minimum and set the launcher to never hide.

If you want the conky config file I can post it.

### Post by viperdvman on 2011-11-23
Like I said I'd probably do before it came out, I waited a month and installed Oneric Ocelot.... on my netbook instead of my desktop. Oneric doesn't seem to like my Graphics setup, so I won't be upgrading until I replace that setup with a single dual-head card.

So far, it's running absolutely great. Sure, it boots up and shuts down a few seconds slower than Natty (only 2-4), but the rest of it runs pretty flawlessly. I can get used to running Unity on the netbook, and I've already played with the panel, launcher, and even menu transparencies. I've even installed the Ambiance Dark Sidebar theme from WebUpd8, which makes the sidebar in Nautilus look more consistent with the title bar and toolbar.

The only problem I have with this release is the same exact problem I had with the Natty... the proprietary ATI driver still sucks at playing online Adobe Flash content (YouTube, Hulu, etc.). I haven't attempted to use the latest drivers yet, as I know one or two more driver updates were released since then. The battery power hasn't improved any since Natty, but it hasn't gotten worse either. I can only hope the Linux kernel developers address this issue and help make Linux as a whole better for laptops.

I've had no problems installing any of my apps that I usually run on the netbook, though I had to search around for how to install Google Chrome. So far, everything runs great, and I'm enjoying playing around with it.

I am keeping Natty on the desktop for 2 reasons: 1) the aforementioned issue with it not booting into Unity but into Unity 2D... it doesn't think my graphics setup is compatible. 2) I still like GNOME 2 and love my Mac OS X style setup. Gonna be hard to pull me away from it since this is my main computer.

Final verdict: I like Oneric Ocelot and I like how much better Unity is becoming. I can't wait to see how much better things will get when Precise Pangolin comes out. :)

---

### Post by oscarivera9 on 2011-12-09
I like the New Ubuntu!
I really liked the last LTS and after I updated from 10.10 to 11.04 I began to consider switching to another distro, perhaps Linux Mint or Debian because I didn't like the direction in which Ubuntu was headed.  However, after updating to 11.10, I am once again pleased to say that Ubuntu rocks!  The previous release was buggy as buggy can be, but a lot of those bugs have been addressed and the result is a more stable New Ubuntu.  I was one of those Unity haters when 11.04 came out, and in fact I continued to use the old Gnome 2.X desktop throughout the entire 11.04 release, but with Oneiric Ocelot, I have finally made the switch to Unity.  I use Ubuntu Studio so after the 11.04 to 11.10 upgrade, I was happy to notice that Xfce was added to my list of desktop environments available to me at startup.  I also went ahead and installed the Gnome 3 desktop environment to try it out.  After a couple of months of trying out three different desktop environments, I came to the conclusion that we in the Ubuntu community are immensely fortunate to have all of these choices, so no matter who you are Ubuntu definitely has something for everyone.  I made the switch to Unity, but along the way I got to try out all sorts of other options.
I will once again say that I  REALLY like the New Ubuntu!!!

:guitar:

### Post by viperdvman on 2011-12-12

Well, you're not exactly "Forced" to use Unity. You can always install GNOME Shell, KDE, Xfce, etc. Or to be rid of Unity completely, just install Kubuntu, Xubuntu, or Lubuntu. All 3 of them have been picking up steam since Unity came out for desktops in 11.04.

That's the beauty of Ubuntu... you're not limited to **just** Unity or GNOME, but rather are given a ton of different desktop environments to choose from. :)
